        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.9b09882"><meta property="og:title" content="Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/acsami.9b09882.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Two-dimensional (2D) bismuth oxide (Bi2O3) heterostructured nanosheets (BOHNs) were first fabricated by a solution-based molecular self-assembly approach. The synthesized BOHNs nanosheets feature mixed α- and β-phases and rich surface/edge-active sites. When utilized as anode materials for rechargeable batteries, dual-phase BOHNs deliver an initial discharge capacity as high as 647.6 mAh g–1 and an increased capacity of over 200 mAh g–1 remained after 260 cycles for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), and a stable cycling capacity at ∼50 mAh g–1 after 500 cycles for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). A novel flexible 2D/1D/2D structure is further developed by implanting 2D BOHNs into conductive 1D carbon nanotubes and 2D graphene to form composite (BOHNCG) paper as free-standing anodes for both LIBs and SIBs. The capacity of 2D/1D/2D BOHNCG as a LIB anode reaches 823.5 mAh g–1, corresponding to an enhancement of ∼27%, and remains at >110 mAh g–1 after 80 cycles as a SIB anode with greatly improved cycling stability. This work verifies the promising potential of 2D BOHNs for practical energy-related devices and enriches the current research on emerging 2D nanomaterials."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/acsami.9b09882.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="931889eaf9a954b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="931889eaf9a954b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '4f183def-d92c-4412-84fc-639bd3cec4ee'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="931889eaf9a954b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b09882&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b09882&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b09882" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="931889eaf9a954b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Two-Dimensional Bismuth Oxide Heterostructured Nanosheets for Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Storages", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b09882", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">68</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/medium/am-2019-09882n_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b09882&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Two-dimensional (2D) bismuth oxide (Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) heterostructured nanosheets (BOHNs) were first fabricated by a solution-based molecular self-assembly approach. The synthesized BOHNs nanosheets feature mixed α- and β-phases and rich surface/edge-active sites. When utilized as anode materials for rechargeable batteries, dual-phase BOHNs deliver an initial discharge capacity as high as 647.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and an increased capacity of over 200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> remained after 260 cycles for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), and a stable cycling capacity at ∼50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). A novel flexible 2D/1D/2D structure is further developed by implanting 2D BOHNs into conductive 1D carbon nanotubes and 2D graphene to form composite (BOHNCG) paper as free-standing anodes for both LIBs and SIBs. The capacity of 2D/1D/2D BOHNCG as a LIB anode reaches 823.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to an enhancement of ∼27%, and remains at &gt;110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 80 cycles as a SIB anode with greatly improved cycling stability. This work verifies the promising potential of 2D BOHNs for practical energy-related devices and enriches the current research on emerging 2D nanomaterials.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D28205%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJun%2BMei%252C%2BTing%2BLiao%252C%2BGodwin%2BA.%2BAyoko%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D31%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b09882%26title%3DTwo-Dimensional%2BBismuth%2BOxide%2BHeterostructured%2BNanosheets%2Bfor%2BLithium-%2Band%2BSodium-Ion%2BStorages%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D28212%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_free-standing">free-standing</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene">graphene</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Recently, two-dimensional (2D) metal oxide nanosheets, as a major member of 2D family, have attracted much attention for energy conversion and storage devices due to their low costs, abundant active sites, excellent chemical activity, fast ion-diffusion rate, good in-plane carrier-/charge-transport kinetics, superior mechanical flexibility, and outstanding tolerance ranges toward various acids/bases. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> Until now, a wide range of metal oxide nanosheets, including binary metal oxides (TiO<sub>2</sub>, Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, NiO, MnO<sub>2</sub>, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, etc.) and ternary metal oxides (Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>, NiCo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, ZnCo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, etc.), have been successfully fabricated via either direct exfoliation from their corresponding bulk counterparts or self-assembly from salt precursor-containing solutions (e.g., metal hydroxide, sulfide, nitrate, and carbonate). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 6 to 12">(6−12)</a> It has been demonstrated that many metal oxide nanosheets exhibit outstanding electrochemical properties toward various types of electrocatalysis reactions and rechargeable batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 13 to 21">(13−21)</a> For example, a novel and interesting layered cobalt oxide nanosheet, recently developed by Hu et al., exhibited an amazing high-rate performance for lithium-ion secondary batteries with ultrafast lithiation kinetics based on a rapid amorphization mechanism. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Bismuth oxide (Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) semiconductor, with a band gap of around 2.5–2.8 eV, has received considerable attention in energy-related fields, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 23 to 31">(23−31)</a> owing to its suitable working window, exceptional theoretical specific capacitance/capacity, and relatively simple synthesis procedure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 32 to 36">(32−36)</a> Generally, Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> possesses five polymorphic crystal forms, including α-, β-, γ-, δ-, and ω-Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. Among them, the stable monoclinic α-phase is the most common phase, while the metastable β-Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> exhibits excellent ion conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Despite the fact that some explorations on the synthesis of this type of metal oxides have been performed, 2D Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> heterostructured nanosheets (BOHNs), to the best of our knowledge, has not been reported yet.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we first report the molecular self-assembly fabrication of 2D BOHNs with the assistance of polymer-based surfactants. By employing lamellar reverse micelles, the confined growth of 2D BOHNs can be effectively achieved, and this synthetic approach has a great potential for scale-up production. The resulting dual-phase BOHNs nanosheets feature mixed crystals of both α- and β-phases and rich surface/edge-active sites, which can significantly enhance the surface redox and/or interfacial capacitance for ion storage. When utilized as anode materials for rechargeable batteries, these nanosheets deliver a high initial discharge capacity of 647.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a reversible capacity of over 200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 260 cycles for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), and a stable cycling capacity at ∼50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). To further improve its electrical conductivity, the 2D BOHNs was embedded into a flexible conductive carbon nanotubes (CNTs)/graphene hybrid matrix to produce a free-standing ternary heterogeneous hybrid composite (BOHNs/CNTs/graphene, BOHNCG) paper anode. The specific capacity of the binder-free BOHNCG anode for LIBs can be enhanced by ∼27% and the cycling stability for SIBs was considerably improved. These results verify the availability of 2D BOHNs for high-performance energy-related devices and the enrichment of the current research on emerging 2D nanosheets.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Single-layer graphene oxide (GO) aqueous dispersion was provided by Hangzhou Gaoxi Tech Co., Ltd. Pristine carbon nanotubes (CNTs) were purchased from Cnano Tech Co. Ltd. All other chemicals were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich without any further purification. Deionized water used in the experiment was purified by a SAS 67120 water purification system (Millipore, France).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Fabrication of 2D Heterostructured Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> Nanosheets (BOHNs)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">In a typical synthesis, Pluronic P123 (PEO/PPO/PEO = 20:70:20, 620 mg) was dissolved in an ethanol-water mixture at a weight ratio of 18:17 under vigorous magnetic stirring. When the complete dissolution of P123 polymer was achieved, bismuth nitrate (Bi(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O, 390 mg) was added and the transparent solution then turned to milky white. Subsequently, ethylene glycol (EG, 36 mL) was added with the appearance of a homogeneous transparent solution after stirring for another 30 min. Subsequently, the precursor solution was aged for 24 h at room temperature before being transferred into autoclaves and then treated at 170 °C for 1–5 h. After the autoclave was cooled down, the powders were collected after centrifuging, water washing, and air-drying at 65 °C. The 2D BOHNs were finally obtained by the thermal treatment of the obtained powders at 400 °C for 30 min under argon and air flow.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Fabrication of BOHNs/CNTs/Graphene Composite (BOHNCG) Paper</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Single-layer GO aqueous suspension (∼2.5 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) and surfactant-stabilized CNTs suspension (∼2.0 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) were homogeneously mixed based on a weight ratio of 1:5 under ultrasonic treatment. Following that, 2D BOHNs were added into the graphene/CNT suspension and treated under ultrasonication for another 1 h. Subsequently, the mixed suspensions were vacuum-filtrated through a mixed cellulose membrane. The free-standing BOHNCG paper was finally obtained after annealing the filtered BOHNs/CNTs/graphene filtered film at 400 °C for 30 min under argon flow.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Morphology and Structural Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Morphologies and microstructures of 2D BOHNs and BOHNCG were examined by a Zeiss Sigma field-emission scanning electron microscope (Germany) and a JEOL 2100 transmission electron microscope (TEM, Japan). Phase compositions of the precursor and 2D BOHNs product were confirmed by X-ray diffraction (XRD) on a Panalytical X’Pert MPD. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR, Bruker Optics ALPHA, Germany) equipped with Eco ATR module was applied to identify the crystal vibrations in 2D BOHNs. Ultraviolet–visible diffuse reflectance spectroscopy (UV–vis/DRS) of 2D BOHNs was recorded on a Cary 5000 by using barium sulfate (BaSO<sub>4</sub>) powder as a referred sample for baseline calibration. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) tests was conducted on a Kratos AXIS Supra photoelectron. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) for BOHNCG paper was performed at a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> on a STA 449F3 Jupiter Simultaneous thermal analyzer (Netzsch, Germany). Raman spectra of the precursor and 2D BOHNs product were recorded on a Renishaw Raman microscope. The thickness of the 2D BOHNs was confirmed by using a NT-MDT Solver Pro atomic force microscope (AFM). Nitrogen adsorption–desorption plots was obtained on a TriStar 3020 analyzer (Micromeritics).</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.5.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To obtain the working electrode, the as-synthesized 2D BOHNs (80 wt %) were homogeneously mixed with carbon black (10 wt %, Timcal) and poly(vinylidene difluoride) binder (10 wt %, Arkema) in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone solvent, and then the mixed slurry was coated on a piece of copper foil. After vacuum drying at 110 °C for 12 h, the foil was punched into small disks as anodes. The average mass loadings of active material on the anodes were ∼0.41 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. In the battery performance evaluation of BOHNCG anodes, free-standing BOHNCG papers were directly used as working electrodes. The CR 2032 coin cells were finally assembled in Ar-filled Mbraun glovebox with metallic lithium or sodium foil as the counter or reference electrode, respectively. For Li-ion batteries, the electrolyte was prepared by dissolving 1 M LiPF<sub>6</sub> salt into a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1:1, v/v). For Na-ion batteries, the electrolyte was prepared by dissolving NaClO<sub>4</sub> salt (1.0 M) into a mixed organic solvent (1:1, v/v) of EC and DEC with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate additives. Electrochemical properties were evaluated on a high-performance battery tester system (Neware, China) in the potential range of 0–3.0 V (vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li) for LIBs and 0.01–2.5 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) for SIBs. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) plots were acquired on a CHI 760 electrochemical workstation. Electrochemical impedance spectra were recorded with an alternating current voltage signal of 5 mV within a frequency range of 0.005–100 000 Hz.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the schematic illustration of the molecular self-assembly of 2D BOHNs, in which the BOHNs were synthesized via a two-step process, including a hydrothermal process at 170 °C for 1–5 h and a thermal treatment at 400 °C for 30 min. During the first step, the amphiphilic P123 block copolymer was dissolved in an ethanol–water mixture system and then bismuth-containing metal salt was introduced to form a milky white sol, which was further modified by short-chain ethylene glycol (EG) alcohol molecules. The exquisite combination of P123 and EG acting as effective co-surfactant system is favorable for the confined growth of the resulting bismuth oxide precursor along the desired direction. It should be stressed that the function of P123 polymer is crucial for the final 2D nanostructure. Without the addition of P123, some nanoparticle aggregations instead of 2D nanostructures were formed in the products (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, Supporting Information). After a subsequent calcination step and the following ultrasonic treatment, well-crystallized 2D BOHNs were obtained. The phase composition and morphology changes before and after calcination were examined by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and scanning electron microscope (SEM), respectively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b–d, after treatment, the sheetlike Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> precursor transformed into dual-phase BOHNs (α-/β-Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>), but the 2D surface morphology was still well maintained. Meanwhile, the color of the corresponding powder changed from light yellow to brown yellow (inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d). It should be also noted that the hydrothermal duration time has some influence on the thickness and morphology of the 2D products. When hydrothermally treated for 2 h, both the Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> precursors and BOHNs were well isolated nanosheets, and the thickness of the resultant BOHNs was around 17 nm as confirmed by atomic force microscope (AFM) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). When the reaction time increased into 5 h, 2D BOHNs nanosheets were stacked into three-dimensional (3D) flowerlike nanostructures, and the thickness of each nanosheet was increased to ∼25 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The distinct sheetlike 2D nanosheet feature of the obtained BOHNs was also confirmed by using a bright-field transmission electron microscope (TEM) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e). High-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>) and the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g,h) clearly verify the presence of heterogeneous structure with the coexistence of both α and β phases in 2D BOHNs. Energy-dispersive spectroscopic (EDS) mappings patterns of bismuth (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>i) and oxygen (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>j) elements of 2D BOHNs demonstrate the homogeneity of the 2D bismuth oxide nanosheets.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/medium/am-2019-09882n_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Fabrication and microstructure of 2D BOHNs. (a) Schematic illustration of the fabrication of BOHNs. (b) XRD patterns of Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> precursor and BOHNs. (c) SEM image of Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> precursor. Inset shows the photo of the as-synthesized white precursor powders. (d) SEM image of BOHNs. Inset shows the yellow BONs powders. (e) Bright-field and (f) high-resolution TEM images of BOHNs. (g, h) Selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns showing the existence of α- and β-phase in BOHNs. (i, j) Energy-dispersive spectroscopic (EDS) element mappings of BOHNs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b09882&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further reveal the chemical composition of the synthesized 2D BOHNs, Raman spectroscopy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a), Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c,d) techniques were employed for more details. Raman analysis indicates that there are three obvious bands at 127.8, 316.7, and 466.6 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which should be attributed to the bismuth–oxygen stretches of Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanostructures. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> However, no such obvious peaks can be found from the Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> precursor nanosheets (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). The crystal structure difference can also be reflected from the FT-IR spectrum of BOHNs. Two intensive peaks located at 1387.1 and 845.9 cm<sup>–1</sup> as well as the peaks in the range of 400–600 cm<sup>–1</sup> can be assigned to the vibrations of the bismuth–oxygen–(bismuth) bonds (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). These results are in good agreement with the reported characteristic spectrometric data of bismuth oxide. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The XPS survey spectrum reveals the presence of mainly bismuth and oxygen (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d shows the Bi 4f XPS spectrum of 2D BOHNs, in which the peaks at 158.6 and 163.7 eV are assigned to Bi 4f<sub>7/2</sub> and Bi 4f<sub>5/2</sub>, respectively. The binding energy gap of 5.1 eV verified the presence of Bi<sup>3+</sup> in the BOHNs. Ultraviolet–visible diffuse reflectance spectrum (UV–vis/DRS) of 2D BOHNs and the corresponding Tauc plot (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) evidences that the resultant nanosheets possess a band gap of ∼2.2 eV. Surface area measurements indicate that the specific surface area of the resulting 2D BOHNs was ∼15 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> together with an average pore diameter of 4.9 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/medium/am-2019-09882n_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Chemical states of 2D BOHNs. (a) Raman spectrum, (b) FT-IR spectrum, (c) XPS survey spectrum, and (d) the high resolution Bi 4f spectrum of of BOHNs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b09882&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Generally, most features of 2D nanomaterials are highly desirable for the applications in electrochemical energy conversion, and storage devices benefited by their significant structural advantages, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> such as high proportion of exposed active surface, short cross-plane diffusion length, ultrafast in-plane mass transport, etc. The obtained 2D BOHNs are expected to be attractive candidates as electrode materials for energy storage devices, such as LIBs and SIBs. Particularly, the as-obtained 2D BOHNs possess some extra merits, such as a dual-phase composition and a narrow band gap. Therefore, the electrochemical performance of this unique material as the anodes of LIBs and SIBs is examined in subsequent sections.</div><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical Li<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of the fabricated 2D BOHNs was first evaluated by assembled as a working anode. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, the discharge capacities of BOHNs were 647.6, 425.2, and 318.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for Li<sup>+</sup> storage in the first three cycles at a low current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. It is noteworthy that the initial capacity of BOHNs is very close to the theoretical capacity of Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (∼690 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), in which 12 Li ions are involved for each unit (Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 12 Li ↔ 3 Li<sub>2</sub>O + 2 Li<sub>3</sub>Bi). The 34.3% of initial capacity loss in the second cycle should be ascribed to the formation of a undesired solid electrolyte interface (SEI), which was also evidenced by the cathodic peak located at ∼0.6 V in the cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of BOHNs (inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). Other cathodic peaks appearing in ∼1.3 and ∼0.75 V are possibly related to the reduction of Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> into metallic Bi and the final formation of Li<sub>3</sub>Bi compound (inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), respectively. On charging, the de-alloying reaction of Li<sub>3</sub>Bi into Bi occurs with a corresponding anodic peak at ∼1.0 V in the CV profiles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 34, 39 and 40">(34,39,40)</a> These results indicate a good reversibility for BOHNs electrode. As the current density increases, the discharge capacities of BOHNs anode start to decrease. The average capacities at the densities of 50 and 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> were close to 240 and 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). When the density increased to 1000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, only 25 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of discharge capacity remained. This rapid decay of capacity has a strong relationship with the inherent electronic conductivity of the Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> semiconductor. Surprisingly, if the density shifted back to the initial 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the BOHNs electrode could maintain up to 100 cycles with a stable Li<sup>+</sup> capacity of ∼50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, confirming its potential for steady electrochemical ion storage. Cycling stability tests (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) demonstrate that the 2D BOHNs electrode presented a rapid decrease in the first 20 cycles and remained stable thereafter continuously to more than 260 discharging/charging cycles with over 210 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of specific capacity achieved after cycles. This should be associated with the formation of metallic bismuth intermediate during cycling, which is in favor of enhanced efficient charge transfer in the electrode through the reaction with Li<sup>+</sup> ions. As evidenced by a comparison of Nyquist plots before and after the cycling tests of 2D BOHNs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d), the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), as indicated by the semicircle in the mid-frequency region, was greatly decreased after cycling. SEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e) and TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f) collected on the 2D nanosheets after cycling further reveal that the 2D sheetlike nanostructures of BOHNs were well maintained. The HR-TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>g) confirms that the α- and β-Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> phases still coexisted in the 2D nanosheets, suggesting an attractive structural integrity of the 2D BOHNs over cycling for electrochemical Li<sup>+</sup> ions storage.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/medium/am-2019-09882n_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of 2D BOHNs as anode materials for LIBs. (a) Charge and discharge profiles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Rate capability at an increased current density from 50 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and then back to initial 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Inset shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling stability over 265 discharging/charging cycles. Inset shows the cubic crystal structure of Li<sub>3</sub>Bi product during discharging. (d) Nyquist plots before and after cycles. Inset shows the corresponding equivalent circuit plot. (e) SEM, (f) TEM, and (g) HR-TEM images of 2D BOHNs tested after cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b09882&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">We also explored the potential use of 2D BOHNs as an anode material for Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage in SIBs. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, the discharge capacities for Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the first three cycles were 565.8, 419.4, and 366.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which are even comparable to those for LIBs. In the first 30 cycles, a continuous decreasing tendency was observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). This is primarily due to the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> than that of Li<sup>+</sup> ions, which leads to a sluggish reaction kinetic, a rapid capacity loss, and a severe structural damage of the electrode. With respect to the reaction pathways, as evidenced by the CV curves in the first three cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c), multiple peaks were observed in the cathodic region, which should be attributed to the growth of SEI and the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi discharging product. Similar to LIBs, the obvious peak located at ∼0.75 V in the anodic region corresponded to the de-alloying of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi compound to metallic Bi, which then gradually evolved into Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> as demonstrated by the appearance of a broad peak at ∼1.6 in the CV curves. After 50 cycles, the capacity of BOHNs maintained a stable level at ∼50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for up to 500 cycles. Different from LIB, a considerable increase in <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the 2D BOHNs anode was observed after cycles, as shown in the Nyquist plots (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d). SEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e), TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f), the corresponding element mappings of Bi (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>g) and O (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>h), and HR-TEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>i) proved that the morphology, the electrode structure, and the phase composition of 2D BOHNs were well-retained after cycles.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/medium/am-2019-09882n_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of 2D BOHNs as anode materials for SIBs. (a) Charge and discharge profiles of BOHNs anode at 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Cycling performance of BOHNs at a current density of 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 500 cycles. (c) CV profiles at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the first three cycles. (d) Nyquist plots before and after cycles. Inset shows the corresponding equivalent circuit plot. (e) SEM images, (f) BF-TEM image, and the corresponding element mappings of (g) Bi and (h) O. (i) HR-TEM image shows the coexistence of dual phase of Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> after cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b09882&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Even though BOHNs have presented significant potential for energy storage applications as evidenced by its high initial discharge capacity mentioned above, the rapid fading of the specific capacity aroused by the intrinsic low electronic conductivity and inadequate active sites for anchoring ions of Bi<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> yet impedes its practical electrochemical application of this material. To further promote the electrochemical properties for ions storage, a novel BOHNs-based flexible and conductive hybrid paper anode was fabricated to form a 2D/1D/2D structure by vacuum filtering the mixed dispersion composed of the as-synthesized 2D BOHNs, graphene, and CNTs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a) for addressing the main issues, especially the intrinsic low electronic conductivity, of 2D BOHNs for LIBs and SIBs. It has also been reported that the 2D–2D heterostructures or homostructures allow ultrafast interlayer charge/mass transport, which can contribute to improving the performance of energy devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The obtained ternary BOHNs/CNTs/graphene composite (BOHNCG) paper possessed a thickness of ∼7.5 μm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b) and an approximate weight percentage of 70% for 2D BOHNs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). Due to the presence of BOHNs, it is clearly observed that some pores were formed between the graphene interlayers (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), which can favor electrolyte infiltration and ion diffusion for battery applications. Besides the porous structure, the free-standing paper electrode exhibited a slight rough surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c) with a uniform distribution of bismuth, oxygen, and carbon elements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10</a>). TEM images further reveal that a unique 2D/1D/2D heterogeneous structure was constructed by supporting 2D BOHNs and 1D CNTs onto 2D graphene nanosheets (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e,f). In this design, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>g, a good conductive network can be built over the whole paper framework. Moreover, this 2D/1D/2D configuration allows an enhanced chemical reactivity contributed by the large-area exposed surfaces, the abundant defect-rich edges, and the confined interlayers. This structure can also significantly decrease the resistance owing to the existence of conductive graphene and CNTs networks. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 42 to 44">(42−44)</a> In the meantime, self-aggregation of the individual large-plane-size 2D graphene, large-aspect-ratio 1D CNTs, and 2D BOHNs can be effectively avoided through the formation of this 2D/1D/2D structure. As a flexible and binder-free electrode for batteries, the possible volume change of bismuth oxide nanosheets can be effectively alleviated, and thus the appearance of serious powder pulverization process upon cycling can be avoided. The innovative 2D–1D–2D configuration thus is highly expected to effectively increase reversible capacity, enhance rate capability, and cycling stability for both LIBs and SIBs.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/medium/am-2019-09882n_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Morphology and structural characterization of BOHNs/CNTs/graphene composite (BOHNCG) paper and its electrochemical performance as free-standing anodes for LIBs and SIBs. (a) Optical photograph of BOHNCG paper showing its flexibility. (b) Sectional SEM image of BOHNCG paper. (c) SEM image of BOHNCG paper surface and (d) the corresponding element mapping distribution. (e, f) TEM image of BOHNCG paper showing the presence of 2D graphene, 1D CNTs, and 2D BOHNs. Inset in (e) shows the schematic illustration of 2D/1D/2D heterogeneous structure in the BOHNCG paper. (g) Schematic illustration of the structural advantages of the BONCG hybrid paper for ions storage. (h) CV profiles at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the first four cycles, (i) charge and discharge profiles in the first three cycles, and (j) rate capability of free-standing BOHNCG paper anode for LIBs. (k) CV profiles at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the first five cycles, (l) charge and discharge profiles in the first three cycles, and (m) cycle stability at a rate of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> of free-standing BOHNCG paper anode for SIBs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/asset/images/large/am-2019-09882n_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b09882&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">As expected, compared to monolithic 2D BOHNs, the electrochemical properties of the free-standing BOHNCG paper for both LIBs and SIBs are considerably improved. For Li<sup>+</sup> ions storage, the growth of SEI occurred at a lower voltage (&lt;0.5 V) and the anodic peaks were relatively stable in the first three cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>h). The initial discharge capacity of the BOHNCG paper anode was enhanced to 823.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which corresponds to a capacity increase of ∼27% compared to BOHNs. Surprisingly, in the following cycles, the Coulombic efficiencies of the BOHNCG anode were close to 100% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>i), indicating a high degree of reversibility in phase-transition process over discharge/charge. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>j, the average capacities of the BOHNCG anode were close to 300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a rate of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Interestingly, when the rate increased to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, over 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> of the specific capacity remained, which is much better than that of the individual 2D BOHNs. If utilized as an anode material for SIBs, the free-standing BOHNCG paper presented similar but much improved CV behaviors to that of the individual 2D BOHNs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>k) with the Coulombic efficiencies of ∼100% from the second cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>l). It is worth noting that the capacity remained around 110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 80 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>m), which is extremely superior to the individual 2D BOHNs anode. After cycling tests, no obvious morphology changes of BOHNCG anodes were identified for both LIBs and SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). Furthermore, the electrochemical performance of the free-standing BOHNCG paper electrode is comparable to those reported for bismuth oxide/carbon composite anodes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40 ref45" aria-label="reference 39, 40 and 45">(39,40,45)</a> confirming its availability for practical energy storage devices.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">2D bismuth oxide nanosheets were fabricated by a solution-based molecular self-assembly approach for the first time. When explored as potential anode materials for high-performance lithium and sodium ion batteries, the 2D BOHNs delivered a high capacity close to the theoretical capacity (647.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and 565.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for SIBs). Furthermore, the battery performance can be further enhanced by the design of a novel flexible free-standing BOHNs/CNTs/graphene hybrid paper anode. Depending on this innovative 2D/1D/2D architecture, the specific capacity of the BOHNCG paper anode was enhanced by ∼27% for LIBs and a much less capacity decay for SIBs, respectively, which exhibits great potential as an exceptional anode material for practical rechargeable batteries. This research also enriches the library of the emerging 2D nanomaterials and opens a new pathway for the design of novel heterogeneous free-standing electrodes for high-performance energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b09882" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b09882</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">TEM images, SEM images, AFM images, Raman spectra, UV–vis/DRS spectra, TGA curves, and nitrogen adsorption–desorption plots of 2D BOHNs and/or BOHNCG anodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b09882/suppl_file/am9b09882_si_001.pdf">am9b09882_si_001.pdf (588.61 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b09882" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Two-dimensional (2D) nanomaterials show unique elec., mech., and catalytic performance owing to their ultrahigh surface-to-vol. ratio and quantum confinement effects.  However, ways to simply synthesize 2D metal oxide nanosheets through a general and facile method is still a big challenge.  The authors report a generalized and facile strategy to synthesize large-size ultrathin 2D metal oxide nanosheets by using graphene oxide (GO) as a template in a wet-chem. system.  Notably, the novel strategy mainly relies on accurately controlling the balance between heterogeneous growth and nucleation of metal oxides on the surface of GO, which is independent on the individual character of the metal elements.  Therefore, ultrathin nanosheets of various metal oxides, including those from both main-group and transition elements, can be synthesized with large size.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Two-dimensional (2D) transition metal oxide systems present exotic electronic properties and high sp. surface areas, and also demonstrate promising applications ranging from electronics to energy storage.  Yet, in contrast to other types of nanostructures, the question as to whether we could assemble 2D nanomaterials with an at. thickness from mols. in a general way, which may give them some interesting properties such as those of graphene, still remains unresolved.  Herein, we report a generalized and fundamental approach to mol. self-assembly synthesis of ultrathin 2D nanosheets of transition metal oxides by rationally employing lamellar reverse micelles.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Holey two-dimensional transition metal oxide nanosheets for efficient energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Peng Lele; Xiong Pan; Zhu Yue; Chen Dahong; Yu Guihua; Ma Lu; Yuan Yifei; Luo Xiangyi; Lu Jun; Amine Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal oxide nanomaterials are promising electrodes for alkali-ion batteries owing to their distinct reaction mechanism, abundant active sites and shortened ion diffusion distance.  However, detailed conversion reaction processes in terms of the oxidation state evolution and chemical/mechanical stability of the electrodes are still poorly understood.  Herein we explore a general synthetic strategy for versatile synthesis of various holey transition metal oxide nanosheets with adjustable hole sizes that enable greatly enhanced alkali-ion storage properties.  We employ in-situ transmission electron microscopy and operando X-ray absorption structures to study the mechanical properties, morphology evolution and oxidation state changes during electrochemical processes.  We find that these holey oxide nanosheets exhibit strong mechanical stability inherited from graphene oxide, displaying minimal structural changes during lithiation/delithiation processes.  These holey oxide nanosheets represent a promising material platform for in-situ probing the electrochemical processes, and could open up opportunities in many energy storage and conversion systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.pmatsci.2019.03.001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mei, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ayoko, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review. If hybridized with property-complementary nanomaterials, such as nanocarbon, CNTs, graphene, metal oxides/sulfides and conductive polymers, their electrochem. properties can be further enhanced in terms of specific reversible capacity/capacitance, rate capability, cycling stability, and catalytic activity.  In this review, we first give a comprehensive overview on recent progress in both monolithic cobalt oxide nanostructures and their hybrid nanomaterials for batteries, supercapacitors, and electrocatalysis applications.
